reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a depthwise 2D convolution with a square input and an asymmetric kernel.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        kernel_size (int): Size of the convolution kernel.
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (int, optional): Padding applied to the input. Defaults to 0.
        dilation (int, optional): Spacing between kernel elements. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, dilation: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv2d = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, in_channels, kernel_size=(kernel_size, 1), stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, groups=in_channels, bias=bias)
        
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the depthwise 2D convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, height_out, width_out).
        """
        return self.conv2d(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 64
in_channels = 8
kernel_size = 3
width = 512
height = 512
stride = 1
padding = 0
dilation = 1

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, dilation]

Since output is NCHW layout. One way: int n = idx / (C*H_out*W_out); int rem = idx % (C*H_out*W_out); int c = rem / (H_out*W_out); rem = rem % (H_out*W_out); int h = rem / W_out; int w = rem % W_out; Then compute: float sum = bias ? bias[c] : 0.0f; int h_in = h * stride - padding; if (h_in >=0 && h_in < H) { // because kernel_height=1 int base_offset = ((n*C + c)*H + h_in)*W; for (int kw=0; kw<kernel_size; ++kw) { int w_in = w * stride - padding + kw * dilation; if (w_in >=0 && w_in < W) { float val = input[base_offset + w_in]; float wgt = weight[((c*1 + 0)*kernel_size + kw)*1 + 0]; // weight shape (C,1,K,1) sum += val * wgt; } } } output[idx] = sum; This is extremely simple. Alternatively, put the host wrapper in `cpp_sources` and call a `void launch_kernel(...)` declared/defined in `cuda_sources`. That also works. Let's think about the kernel design in more detail. Since the kernel is memory bound, we want to maximize throughput. A few ideas: - Use `float4` loads to process 4 output pixels per thread. This reduces instruction overhead and can improve memory bandwidth utilization by vectorized loads. However, the input width for each output is K elements, and with K=3, reading float4 for input might be tricky because of misalignment and boundary conditions. But we can pad input? We cannot change input. But we can process 4 outputs per thread: for each kw, load 4 consecutive input elements (not necessarily aligned). If the start address is aligned, we can use `float4`. Since W is often a multiple of something, maybe not guaranteed. But we can use `float` and rely on compiler. - Another idea: Each block processes a tile of W (e.g., 128) for a given (n,c,h). Load a tile of input (including halo of K-1) into shared memory. Then each thread computes its output using shared memory. This reduces global memory reads since input tile is loaded once. Since K is small, halo is small. This is a typical 1D convolution optimization. Let's explore a tiled shared memory approach. Tile width (TW) = say 256 threads per block. Then input tile needed: TW + K - 1 (plus any stride/dilation scaling). Since dilation and stride can be >1, we can compute the needed input range. For general case, input indices for output indices in [w_start, w_end) are from `w_in = w * stride - padding + kw * dilation`. The range is `(w_start*stride - padding)` to `((w_end-1)*stride - padding + (K-1)*dilation)`. The tile size in input is (w_end - w_start)*stride + (K-1)*dilation. If stride=1, it's TW + (K-1)*dilation. For K=3, dilation=1 => TW+2. If TW=256, input tile size ~258 floats = ~1KB, well within shared memory. For each (n,c,h), a block loads that input tile into shared memory. Then threads compute outputs. Since there are many (n,c,h) rows, occupancy might be limited by block count, but shared memory usage is tiny so occupancy is high. However, the total number of rows is 262k. If we launch one block per row with 256 threads, that's 262k blocks. On a GPU with maybe 80 SMs, each can run many blocks concurrently. 262k blocks is enough to saturate. But if W_out is small (like 10), then TW=256 is overkill. But the problem likely uses W_out = 510 as in test. However the kernel must be generic. We can dynamically choose tile size or just use thread-per-output. The concern with tiling is extra shared memory latency and `__syncthreads()`. But for memory-bound kernels, shared memory can help if there is significant reuse. Here each input element is read K times (3). Without shared memory, each warp reads input from global memory; due to L1 caching, the second and third reads of the same cache line might hit L1, reducing DRAM traffic. But L1 hit rate depends on cache size and number of warps. With 133M outputs, L1 may not hold all reused data. However, for a given row, warps processing consecutive w_out will read overlapping input. If multiple warps are on the same SM, they may share L1, but not reliably. Still, the effective DRAM traffic might be close to 1 read per output because the cache lines cover 32 floats, and adjacent warps reading shifted data will load new cache lines. Let's estimate: input width is 512 floats. A warp of 32 threads reading 3 floats each (with shift) will load a region of width 32+2=34 floats. That's 2 cache lines (128 bytes each, 32 floats per line). Actually a cache line is 128 bytes = 32 floats. For warp reading w_out = [0,31], input region = [-padding ...]. With padding 0, w_in from 0 to 33 (approx). That's two cache lines (0-31 and 32-63). Next warp reads w_out=[32,63], input region 32-65: also two cache lines (32-63, 64-95). So each warp loads 2 cache lines = 64 floats from DRAM, but only uses 32*3=96 float accesses? Wait, no: each thread loads 3 floats at w_in, w_in+1, w_in+2. Because of coalescing, the warp will load addresses [0..33]. That's 34 floats. Coalesced into 2 128-byte transactions (since addresses span 34 floats = 136 bytes, crossing 2 cache lines). So 2 cache lines loaded = 64 floats. But the warp only uses 32*3=96 float loads? Actually each thread does 3 loads, total 96 loads. But because of memory coalescing, hardware groups them. The loads for kw=0 are at w_in = w_out, so addresses 0..31 => one transaction. kw=1 at 1..32 => addresses 1..32, which may be in the same two lines as before. kw=2 at 2..33 => also in same two lines. So total transactions: 2 cache lines per warp. That's 64 floats * 4 bytes = 256 bytes loaded per warp. The warp produces 32 outputs => 32 floats written = 128 bytes. Total memory traffic per warp = 384 bytes for 32 outputs. This is more than the ideal 8 bytes per output * 32 = 256 bytes. It is not reused across warps unless L1 caches it. If L1 caches warp0's loads, warp1 might get a hit for the overlapping line. But warp1 may be scheduled much later, after warp0's data is evicted. Let's draft the CUDA code. ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void depthwise_conv2d_k1_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation, bool bias_flag) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; int CHW_out = C * H_out * W_out; int n = idx / CHW_out; int rem = idx % CHW_out; int HW_out = H_out * W_out; int c = rem / HW_out; rem = rem % HW_out; int h = rem / W_out; int w = rem % W_out; scalar_t sum = bias_flag ? bias[c] : static_cast<scalar_t>(0); int h_in = h * stride - padding; if (h_in >= 0 && h_in < H) { int base_offset = ((n * C + c) * H + h_in) * W; for (int kw = 0; kw < K; ++kw) { int w_in = w * stride - padding + kw * dilation; if (w_in >= 0 && w_in < W) { scalar_t val = input[base_offset + w_in]; scalar_t wgt = weight[((c * 1) * K + kw) * 1]; // shape (C,1,K,1) sum += val * wgt; } } } output[idx] = sum; } ``` Wait, weight indexing: `weight[((c * 1) * K + kw) * 1]`. Since weight shape is (C, 1, K, 1), the stride for dimension 0 is 1*K*1 = K. Actually in NCHW contiguous layout, index = ((c * 1 + 0) * K + kw) * 1 + 0 = c*K + kw. So `weight[c * K + kw]`. Good. Similarly, bias shape (C), index = c. Base offset for input: input is (N,C,H,W) contiguous, so offset = ((n*C + c)*H + h_in)*W + w_in. Now, check output shape: The reference `nn.Conv2d` with these parameters yields output shape computed by PyTorch. We need to compute `H_out` and `W_out` in the host wrapper same as PyTorch. The formula for output size of conv2d: ``` H_out = floor((H + 2*padding - dilation*(kernel_height - 1) - 1) / stride + 1) W_out = floor((W + 2*padding - dilation*(kernel_width - 1) - 1) / stride + 1) ``` Here kernel_height = 1, kernel_width = K. So: ``` H_out = (H + 2*padding - 1) / stride + 1? Wait: dilation*(1-1)=0. So H_out = floor((H + 2*padding - 0 - 1) / stride + 1) = floor((H + 2*padding - 1) / stride + 1). Since all values are positive ints, we can compute: ``` H_out = (H + 2*padding - 1) / stride + 1? Wait, let's expand: floor((H + 2*P - 1) / S) + 1. ``` But is that always integer? Let's test with H=512, P=0, S=1: (512-1)/1 +1 = 511+1=512. Correct. With H=5, P=1, S=1: (5+2-1)/1 +1 = 6+1=7? But careful: if `H + 2*padding - 1` could be negative? No, H>=1, padding>=0. Actually generic formula: `out = (in + 2*padding - dilation*(kernel_size - 1) - 1) / stride + 1`. For kernel_height=1: `out_h = (H + 2*padding - 1) / stride + 1`. But PyTorch's formula uses integer floor division. In C++ integer division truncates toward zero, which for positive numbers is floor. So it's fine. Now, launch configuration: We can compute total outputs = N * C * H_out * W_out. Block size = 256 or 512. Let's use 256 as default. Grid = (total + block_size - 1) / block_size. Host wrapper: ```cpp at::Tensor depthwise_conv2d_k1_forward( const at::Tensor& input, const at::Tensor& weight, const at::Tensor& bias, int64_t stride, int64_t padding, int64_t dilation, bool bias_flag) { // Ensure tensors are contiguous and on CUDA auto input_c = input.contiguous(); auto weight_c = weight.contiguous(); auto bias_c = bias_flag ? bias.contiguous() : bias; // if no bias, bias is undefined maybe // ... } ``` But wait: The reference model uses `bias` argument to nn.Conv2d. The host wrapper can compute sizes and allocate output. Let's write host wrapper in CUDA source: ```cpp at::Tensor depthwise_conv2d_k1_forward( const at::Tensor& input, const at::Tensor& weight, const at::Tensor& bias, int64_t stride, int64_t padding, int64_t dilation, bool bias_flag) { // Check input dims int N = input.size(0); int C = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int K = weight.size(2); // kernel width // weight shape: (C, 1, K, 1) int H_out = (H + 2 * padding - 1) / stride + 1; int W_out = (W + 2 * padding - dilation * (K - 1) - 1) / stride + 1; auto output = at::empty({N, C, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); int total = N * C * H_out * W_out; const int block_size = 256; const int grid_size = (total + block_size - 1) / block_size; AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(input.scalar_type(), "depthwise_conv2d_k1", [&] { const scalar_t* input_ptr = input.data_ptr<scalar_t>(); const scalar_t* weight_ptr = weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(); const scalar_t* bias_ptr = bias_flag ? bias.data_ptr<scalar_t>() : nullptr; scalar_t* output_ptr = output.data_ptr<scalar_t>(); depthwise_conv2d_k1_kernel<scalar_t><<<grid_size, block_size, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( input_ptr, weight_ptr, bias_ptr, output_ptr, N, C, H, W, H_out, W_out, K, static_cast<int>(stride), static_cast<int>(padding), static_cast<int>(dilation), bias_flag ); }); return output; } ``` Wait, `input.contiguous()`? So kernel is height=K, width=1. That means it slides along height, and width dimension just has 1 element. So it's a vertical 1D convolution. I need to re-evaluate everything. Let's look at the test code: width=512, height=512, kernel_size=3. Input: (64, 8, 512, 512). Conv2d with kernel=(3,1), stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1. Output height: floor((512 + 0 - 1*(3-1) -1)/1 +1) = floor((512 -2 -1) +1) = floor(510) = 510. Output width: floor((512 +0 - 1*(1-1) -1)/1 +1) = floor((512 -0 -1)+1) = floor(512) = 512. So output shape: (64,8,510,512). It shrinks height, leaves width unchanged. So the 1D convolution is along the height dimension. This changes memory access pattern: each output element reads K consecutive elements along height. In memory, height is the slow-changing dimension after channel. So consecutive elements along width are contiguous in memory. For a given (n,c,h), the row along width is contiguous. But the kernel accesses along height: for a fixed (n,c,w_out), it reads at h_in = h_out*stride - padding + kh*dilation, and w_in = w_out*stride - padding + 0*dilation = w_out*stride - padding. So for a given w_out and a given h_out, each thread reads at base offset = ((n*C + c)*H + h_in)*W + w_in. Since h_in differs by stride between adjacent output rows, and w_in is constant for a given w_out. The memory access within a warp: if threads compute consecutive h_out for same w_out? Or consecutive w_out for same h_out? Let's think. Memory coalescing is best when threads in a warp access consecutive memory addresses. In the NCHW layout, for fixed n,c,h, addresses increase with w. So if threads in a warp process consecutive w_out for the same h_out, then wIn = w_out*stride - padding (since kernel width=1). So addresses are consecutive in memory (coalesced). However, each such thread needs to read K input elements along height: for each kh, h_in = h_out*stride - padding + kh*dilation. So for each kh, the address is base + w_in, where base changes by H*W per step? Wait, offset = ((n*C + c)*H + h_in)*W + w_in. For a fixed n,c,w_in, varying h_in by 1 changes offset by W. So along height, stride between elements is W floats. Thus, for a thread computing a single output at (h_out,w_out), it performs K loads at offsets separated by W floats. Since W may be large (512), these loads are not contiguous within a thread. But across threads in a warp processing the same h_out and consecutive w_out, for a given kh, the loads are contiguous across w_out because offset = ... + w_in, and w_in increments by 1 per thread. So each kh load is coalesced. The K loads are K separate coalesced loads per warp. That's fine. Alternatively, if threads process consecutive h_out (same w_out), then for a given kh, the offsets differ by stride*W per thread. If stride=1, that means addresses differ by W (512 floats = 2048 bytes). That may still be coalesced if the warp reads a strided pattern; on modern GPUs, strided loads within a warp are coalesced into multiple transactions. Specifically, if each thread accesses an address separated by 2048 bytes, that's 32 threads * 2048 = 65536 bytes span. That's not great. It will require many transactions. So processing consecutive w_out per warp is much better for memory coalescing. Thus the kernel should be organized so that threads within a warp process consecutive w_out for the same (n,c,h_out). That means we want the inner dimension of thread mapping to be w_out. In our 1D thread mapping, if we linearize (n,c,h_out,w_out) in row-major order (w_out fastest), then threads with consecutive idx have consecutive w_out (until w_out wraps). That is perfect: consecutive threads in a warp (threadIdx.x differing by 1) will have consecutive w_out (if blockDim.x is a multiple of 32 and W_out is large enough). Let's verify. If we map idx to (n,c,h,w) with w fastest, then: ``` int w = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int h = tmp % H_out; tmp = tmp / H_out; int c = tmp % C; int n = tmp / C; ``` In this ordering, w_out varies fastest. For consecutive idx, w increments by 1. When w wraps around (at W_out boundary), h increments. So within a warp of 32 threads, as long as the block of threads doesn't cross the W_out boundary, all threads have same n,c,h and consecutive w. Since W_out=512, and block size maybe 256, a warp of 32 threads will always be within the same n,c,h (since 32 < 512). So loads for each kh will be perfectly coalesced. Great. So we should use this ordering for index decomposition. However, division by W_out and H_out and C are needed. But as before, the integer division cost is small relative to memory. We can also map blocks to tiles of w_out. For example, a block handles a (n,c,h) and a chunk of w_out. Then threads don't need the costly divide for w: w = block_start_w + threadIdx.x. But we still need to compute n,c,h for each block. We could use a 3D grid or 2D grid. Option A: 1D grid with blockDim=256. Decompose idx as above. Good. Option B: 2D grid: blockIdx.x corresponds to (n,c,h), blockIdx.y corresponds to w chunks. Within a block, threadIdx.x maps to w relative to chunk. Then minimal decode. Block count for B: total blocks = (N*C*H_out) * ceil(W_out / blockDim.x). For our numbers: N*C*H_out = 64*8*510 = 261,120. W_out=512, blockDim.x=256 => 2 blocks per row. Total blocks = 522,240. That's okay. The advantage of B is fewer integer ops per thread. The disadvantage is that the grid has many blocks but not too many. Actually both are fine. Let's implement B for better coalescing guarantee and less decode. Grid mapping: ``` int block_id = blockIdx.x; // corresponds to linear index over (N, C, H_out) int n = block_id / (C * H_out); int rem = block_id % (C * H_out); int c = rem / H_out; int h = rem % H_out; int w = blockIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (w >= W_out) return; ``` blockIdx.y ranges over ceil(W_out / blockDim.x). Since W_out can be up to maybe large, but CUDA gridDim.y max is 65535. For W_out=512, ceil(512/256)=2. For W_out=10000, ceil(10000/256)=40. So y dimension is small. x dimension is N*C*H_out. N*C*H_out could be large: e.g., N=64, C=512, H=1024 => 33 million, which exceeds max gridDim.x (2^31-1 ~ 2 billion). Actually 33 million < 2 billion, so fine. x dimension is okay. But blockIdx.x = 33 million might be okay. The kernel launch overhead is minimal. However, we can also split x dimension into n and (c,h) using 2D: gridDim.x = N, gridDim.y = C*H_out. Another approach: 1D mapping with w fastest is simpler and generic. Let's go with that first. The integer decode overhead is small. For W_out large, consecutive threads have consecutive w, which is coalesced. Thus: - Weight shape: (out_channels, in_channels/groups, kernel_height, kernel_width) = (C, 1, K, 1). - The conv operation: for each output pixel (h_out, w_out): h_in_start = h_out * stride - padding w_in_start = w_out * stride - padding sum over kh=0..K-1: h_in = h_in_start + kh * dilation w_in = w_in_start + 0 * dilation = w_in_start weight[c, 0, kh, 0] So the 1D convolution is along height. That's perfectly coalesced. And the K different kh correspond to K different h_in offsets, each with a coalesced load. This is great. Similarly, output writes are contiguous across w. Thus, mapping with w fastest is ideal. Now, for the index decode, we can do: ``` int HW_out = H_out * W_out; int CHW_out = C * HW_out; int n = idx / CHW_out; int rem = idx % CHW_out; int c = rem / HW_out; rem = rem % HW_out; int h = rem / W_out; int w = rem % W_out; ``` This uses 2 divisions and 2 mods per thread. We can maybe optimize by precomputing CHW_out and HW_out, but we need to pass them as ints. Another optimization: use 64-bit for idx if total > 2^31. N*C*H_out*W_out could be up to >2^31. `int` is 32-bit signed, max ~2.1B. For 64*8*512*512 ≈ 134M, fits in int. For larger inputs maybe still fits. But we can use `int64_t` or `long long` for idx to be safe. The grid can be 1D with up to 2^31-1 blocks? Actually max blocks in x is 2^31-1. But total threads = grid * block. If total > 2^31-1 * 256, that could overflow int. It's safer to use 64-bit indexing inside kernel (`int64_t`). CUDA `blockIdx.x` etc are `int`. So `blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x` is int. For total > 2^31, we might need to cast. But realistically not needed. However, use `int64_t idx = (int64_t)blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;` to be safe. Let's write the kernel with `int64_t`. Now, consider using shared memory for padding and the weight? Actually for this kernel, weight accesses are broadcast (same c, same kh across warp). So L1 broadcast is good. Shared memory for input may not be necessary. But there is another optimization: Since K is small and contiguous along height (stride=W), we could load the K input values into registers with K loads. For a warp, each kh load is coalesced. So total K loads per thread. Number of FMAs = K. For K=3, 3 FMAs. For FP32, that's 6 FLOPs. Memory traffic: K loads of input (but coalesced, effectively ~K/WarpSize*? Wait, each warp for each kh loads a contiguous segment of W floats. The number of bytes loaded per warp per kh = (blockDim.x * stride + something) * 4. If blockDim.x=256, warp reads 32 floats. That's one 128-byte cache line. So for each kh, one cache line per warp. K=3 => 3 cache lines per warp = 384 bytes. For 256 threads (8 warps), that's 24 cache lines = 3KB per block. Output write: 256 floats = 1KB. So total traffic per block ≈ 4KB for 256 outputs. That's 16 bytes per output. Wait, 4KB/256 = 16 bytes. Input read 3KB, output write 1KB. That's 12 bytes + 4 bytes? Wait, input read: 3 cache lines per warp * 8 warps = 24 cache lines = 3072 bytes. Output write: 256 floats = 1024 bytes. Total = 4096 bytes for 256 outputs = 16 bytes per output. But each output only needs 3 input floats + 1 output float = 16 bytes. So there is no reuse across warps in a block unless cache lines cover multiple warps. Actually each warp of 32 threads for kh=0 loads 32 floats = 1 cache line. The next warp for kh=0 loads the next 32 floats = next cache line. So there is no overlap between warps. Total input read = K * blockDim.x floats = 768 floats = 3072 bytes. Output = 256 floats = 1024 bytes. Total = 16 bytes per output. That's exactly the naive traffic: each input element is read K times (no reuse across threads). Wait, but if K=3, each input element is used by up to 3 output positions along height, not along width. Since we are processing w_out fastest, adjacent threads in a warp vary w, not h. So they read from the same h_in row. Within a row, each input element is read only once (by the thread computing the output at that w). For K=3, each thread reads 3 different h_in rows. There is no overlap in input reads across threads (except for the 0-d overlap due to cache line granularity). So the total input traffic per block is indeed K * blockDim.x floats. That's unavoidable without tiling across h dimension. But if we tile across h (e.g., a block processes a 2D tile of h and w), we could exploit the overlap along h: each input row is used by up to K output rows. So a block processing a tile of TH x TW outputs would read TH+K-1 rows, each of width TW. Total input loads = (TH+K-1)*TW floats. Outputs = TH*TW. Traffic per output = (TH+K-1)/TH * 4 bytes read + 4 bytes write. For TH=8, (8+2)/8 = 1.25 => 5 bytes read + 4 write = 9 bytes per output. For TH=1, 3*4=12 bytes read + 4 write = 16 bytes per output. So tiling across h can reduce read bandwidth by factor ~TH/(TH+K-1). For TH=8, factor 8/10=0.8. Not huge but maybe significant. However, with K=3, and reading 3 rows per thread, if W_out=512, each row is 512 floats = 2KB. A block of 256 threads processing a tile (TH=8, TW=64) might be nice. But then we need to map threads to positions in the tile. That adds complexity. But maybe L2 cache already captures reuse across blocks when they process consecutive h. Since H_out=510, and blocks with same w but different h are launched sequentially, L2 might retain the row for some time. Actually, each block reads K rows. The next block (h+1) reads rows h+1 to h+K. The overlap is K-1 rows. If the L2 cache is large enough to hold a few rows, then the redundant loads are served from L2, not DRAM. Since each row is 2KB, L2 is many MB. blocks processing consecutive h will have overlapping rows in L2. So effective DRAM traffic might be close to 1 read per output element plus overhead. The naive kernel may actually be close to optimal in practice because L2 caches the rows. While a tile in shared memory guarantees it, the L2 may already do a good job. Now, let's write the Python file. We need to define `ModelNew` class. Inside `ModelNew.__init__`, we need to store the parameters. We can create the conv2d module but not use it in forward. Or we can just create the parameters directly. Simpler: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1, bias=False): super().__init__() self.in_channels = in_channels self.kernel_size = kernel_size self.stride = stride self.padding = padding self.dilation = dilation self.bias_flag = bias self.weight = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(in_channels, 1, kernel_size, 1)) if bias: self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(in_channels)) else: self.register_parameter('bias', None) self.reset_parameters() def reset_parameters(self): nn.init.kaiming_uniform_(self.weight, a=math.sqrt(5)) if self.bias is not None: fan_in, _ = nn.init._calculate_fan_in_and_fan_out(self.weight) bound = 1 / math.sqrt(fan_in) nn.init.uniform_(self.bias, -bound, bound) ``` But we need to make sure that the weight parameter shape and initialization match `nn.Conv2d`. Yes. Alternatively, simply instantiate `nn.Conv2d` and steal its parameters: ```python conv = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, in_channels, kernel_size=(kernel_size, 1), stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, groups=in_channels, bias=bias) self.weight = conv.weight if bias: self.bias = conv.bias ``` But `self.weight` then is a parameter belonging to `conv` local variable. Good. Now, we need to compile the extension. The extension code: ```python import math import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline _cuda_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void depthwise_conv2d_k1_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation, bool bias_flag) { int64_t idx = static_cast<int64_t>(blockIdx.x) * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int64_t total = static_cast<int64_t>(N) * C * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; int64_t HW_out = static_cast<int64_t>(H_out) * W_out; int64_t CHW_out = static_cast<int64_t>(C) * HW_out; int n = static_cast<int>(idx / CHW_out); int64_t rem = idx % CHW_out; int c = static_cast<int>(rem / HW_out); rem = rem % HW_out; int h = static_cast<int>(rem / W_out); int w = static_cast<int>(rem % W_out); scalar_t acc = bias_flag ? bias[c] : static_cast<scalar_t>(0); int h_in = h * stride - padding; int w_in = w * stride - padding; // kernel width is 1 if (h_in >= 0 && h_in < H && w_in >= 0 && w_in < W) { int base_offset = ((n * C + c) * H + h_in) * W + w_in; for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_curr = h_in + kh * dilation; if (h_curr >= 0 && h_curr < H) { scalar_t val = input[base_offset + (h_curr - h_in) * W]; scalar_t wgt = weight[c * K + kh]; acc += val * wgt; } } } output[idx] = acc; } at::Tensor depthwise_conv2d_k1_forward( const at::Tensor& input, const at::Tensor& weight, const at::Tensor& bias, int64_t stride, int64_t padding, int64_t dilation, bool bias_flag) { int N = input.size(0); int C = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int K = weight.size(2); int H_out = (H + 2 * padding - 1) / stride + 1; // kernel height = K, but here kernel height dimension size is K, kernel width=1. // Wait! Kernel height = K. So output height formula is: // H_out = (H + 2*padding - dilation*(K-1) - 1) / stride + 1 // Output width formula with kernel width=1: // W_out = (W + 2*padding - 1) / stride + 1 // But padding and dilation and stride are scalar arguments applied to both dimensions. int H_out = (H + 2 * padding - dilation * (K - 1) - 1) / stride + 1; int W_out = (W + 2 * padding - 1) / stride + 1; auto output = at::empty({N, C, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); int64_t total = static_cast<int64_t>(N) * C * H_out * W_out; const int block_size = 256; int grid_size = static_cast<int>((total + block_size - 1) / block_size); AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(input.scalar_type(), "depthwise_conv2d_k1", [&] { const scalar_t* input_ptr = input.data_ptr<scalar_t>(); const scalar_t* weight_ptr = weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(); const scalar_t* bias_ptr = bias_flag ? bias.data_ptr<scalar_t>() : nullptr; scalar_t* output_ptr = output.data_ptr<scalar_t>(); depthwise_conv2d_k1_kernel<scalar_t><<<grid_size, block_size, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( input_ptr, weight_ptr, bias_ptr, output_ptr, N, C, H, W, H_out, W_out, K, static_cast<int>(stride), static_cast<int>(padding), static_cast<int>(dilation), bias_flag ); }); return output; } """ _cpp_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> at::Tensor depthwise_conv2d_k1_forward(const at::Tensor& input, const at::Tensor& weight, const at::Tensor& bias, int64_t stride, int64_t padding, int64_t dilation, bool bias_flag); """ _conv_ext = load_inline( name="depthwise_conv2d_k1", cpp_sources=_cpp_source, cuda_sources=_cuda_source, functions=["depthwise_conv2d_k1_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) ``` Wait, but `load_inline` might need `with_cuda=True` or it auto-detects. Actually `load_inline` has argument `with_cuda` but defaults to None, which auto-detects from CUDA source presence. So should be fine. Now, the kernel body: ``` int h_in = h * stride - padding; int w_in = w * stride - padding; // kernel width is 1 ``` Wait, w_in doesn't depend on any kernel loop (kernel width=1). So we can compute it once. Then for each kh, h_curr = h_in + kh * dilation. We need to check if h_curr is within [0, H). Also need to check if w_in is within [0,W). Since w_in is same for all kh, check once. If h_in <0 or h_in >= H? Actually h_in is the top of the kernel window. Since kernel height may extend beyond bottom, we need to check each h_curr individually. But if the top is already out of range, all kh are out? If h_in <0, then some kh may still be >=0 (e.g., h_in=-1, kh=1, dilation=1 => h_curr=0). So we cannot just check h_in. We need to check each h_curr. But kernel width=1, so w_in is fixed. If w_in is out of [0,W), then the entire output is zero (since no valid input width). So we can check w_in once. Similarly, for h dimension, we must check each kh. Let's adjust: ``` int w_in = w * stride - padding; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) { output[idx] = bias_flag ? bias[c] : 0; return; } int h_in_start = h * stride - padding; scalar_t acc = bias_flag ? bias[c] : 0; int base_offset = ((n * C + c) * H + h_in_start) * W + w_in; for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_curr = h_in_start + kh * dilation; if (h_curr >= 0 && h_curr < H) { scalar_t val = input[base_offset + kh * dilation * W]; scalar_t wgt = weight[c * K + kh]; acc += val * wgt; } } output[idx] = acc; ``` Wait, `base_offset` is computed with `h_in_start`. But if `h_in_start` is negative, `base_offset` could be negative. We should compute `base_offset` only when needed, or offset from a valid base. However, we only access `base_offset + kh * dilation * W` inside the `if (h_curr >= 0 && h_curr < H)` block. If `h_in_start` is negative, we must not dereference `base_offset` because it's out of bounds. So compute `offset = ((n*C + c)*H + h_curr)*W + w_in` inside the loop. That's safest. Simplify: ``` int w_in = w * stride - padding; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) { output[idx] = bias_flag ? bias[c] : 0; return; } scalar_t acc = bias_flag ? bias[c] : 0; int nch_offset = (n * C + c) * H; int h_in_start = h * stride - padding; for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_curr = h_in_start + kh * dilation; if (h_curr >= 0 && h_curr < H) { scalar_t val = input[(nch_offset + h_curr) * W + w_in]; scalar_t wgt = weight[c * K + kh]; acc += val * wgt; } } output[idx] = acc; ``` This is clean. `nch_offset` is same for all kh. No need for `__syncthreads` or shared memory. Now, occupancy: each thread uses a few registers. Should be high. Potential performance issue: the `if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W)` branch will cause divergence only at the edges of width (if padding > 0). With padding=0, w_in is always valid. The compiler may still generate it. The `if (h_curr >=0 && h_curr < H)` branch also only at top/bottom edges. For most outputs, it's always true. For K=3 and H_out=510, the outputs with h=0 and h=509 are edge cases. That's negligible. But there is a subtlety: the condition `h_curr < H` is always true for h in [0, H_out-1] when padding=0 and stride=1, because h_curr max = h + (K-1)*dilation = h + 2. For h up to H_out-1 = H-K, h+2 <= H-1 < H. So always true for h <= H-K. With H_out=510 and H=512, h ranges 0..509. For h=509, h+2=511 < 512. So yes, always true. For h=510 if it existed, false. So for this test case, all threads take the branch. The compiler might still keep the branch, but it's predictable. Thus the kernel body is essentially: ``` scalar_t acc = bias ? bias[c] : 0; int nch_offset = (n*C + c)*H; int h_in = h*stride - padding; int w_in = w*stride - padding; #pragma unroll for (int kh=0; kh<K; ++kh) { scalar_t val = input[(nch_offset + h_in + kh*dilation) * W + w_in]; scalar_t wgt = weight[c*K + kh]; acc += val * wgt; } output[idx] = acc; ``` But K is not compile-time constant, so `#pragma unroll` may unroll partially if K is small? The compiler sees K as a runtime variable loaded from constant memory (argument). It may not unroll. But we could add `pragma unroll 8` to unroll up to 8 iterations. Since K is small, this is good. However, the actual trip count is K, and if K > 8, the rest are not unrolled. But typical kernel_size is small. Let's use `#pragma unroll 8`. Wait, actually `K` is passed as an argument. `pragma unroll` without a number tells the compiler to fully unroll if the trip count is known at compile time. Since it's not, it won't unroll. We can use `pragma unroll 8` to unroll 8 times, but if K is variable, it will generate a loop with up to 8 unrolled iterations and a remainder. But if K is e.g. 3, it will unroll 3 times? Actually the compiler doesn't know K at compile time, so it will generate an unrolled body of 8 and a loop. But if K is passed as a kernel argument, the compiler may not know it's <=8. However, we can use a switch statement for common K values (e.g., 1..7) to call specialized templates. But that's a lot. Given K is typically small (3, 5, 7), maybe the loop overhead is negligible compared to memory latency. But unrolling could help hide latency. We can try `pragma unroll 8` and see. Alternatively, we can manually unroll with a `switch(K)` and call the kernel with template parameter `K`. But we need to compile many instantiations. Not worth it for now. Let's think about another thing: the reference uses `nn.Conv2d` which is heavily optimized. For a small depthwise conv, the overhead of launching our custom kernel might be smaller. However, our kernel might be slower than cuDNN's kernel if cuDNN uses shared memory or registers in a smarter way. I recall that on some GPUs, cuDNN depthwise conv can be slow for small channel counts because it uses generic algorithms. A simple custom kernel often wins. But we can also consider using `shared memory` to prefetch a slice of input. Let's think if that would be easy and beneficial. If we have a block of threads processing consecutive w_out for a single (n,c,h_out). But our mapping is 1D over all outputs, so threads in a block may span multiple (n,c,h). We could restructure the kernel so that each block processes one (n,c,h) and a chunk of w_out. Then all threads in the block have the same n,c,h_in_start (for a given kh). Then for each kh, the threads load a contiguous chunk of input. We can load this chunk into shared memory and compute. But loading into shared memory requires each thread to load some elements. However, since each thread is already loading the same elements from global memory, shared memory doesn't reduce global memory traffic significantly compared to L1 caching? Actually it does ensure that the loads happen only once per block, while with L1 caching they might happen once per warp but also might be evicted. For a block of 256 threads processing 256 w_out, contiguous input is W*K floats. Each thread reads K floats. Without shm, each thread loads directly from global (coalesced). The hardware will coalesce the loads, but each thread still issues the load instruction. With shm, we have fewer load instructions (one per element). But the main bottleneck is DRAM bandwidth and latency. If the input is in L2/L1, the load latency is low. But for K=3, each block reads 768 floats = 3KB. That easily fits in L1. So L1 will cache it. The advantage of shm is minimal. However, if W_out is large and block size is 256, with many blocks on the same SM, the L1 may hold multiple blocks' data. The overlap of rows between blocks (same w range, adjacent h) is K-1 rows. That is 2 rows = 2 * W * 4 bytes = 4KB for W=512. L1 of 128KB can hold many such rows. So L2/L1 caching should capture reuse across adjacent blocks. So DRAM traffic is close to ideal. Thus simple kernel is likely very good. But let's also consider register pressure. Each thread has: idx, total, HW_out, CHW_out, n, rem, c, h, w, acc, h_in, w_in, nch_offset, kh, h_curr, val, wgt. That's maybe 15-20 registers. With block size 256, registers per block ~ 256*20 = 5120. That's very low. Occupancy will be limited by block count, not registers. Wait, register limit per SM is 64K. With 20 registers per thread, a block of 256 threads uses 5120 registers. We can have up to 32 blocks per SM (max blocks per SM is 32 on newer GPUs). 32*5K = 160K > 64K, so register limit allows 12 blocks. So occupancy is high. Thus the kernel should launch many blocks concurrently. Let's code this in Python and test. One more thing: `load_inline` might conflict with naming if we re-run. Use a unique name. Also, we need to include `#include <ATen/cuda/CUDAContext.h>` for `at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()`. I'll write the full Python source for `ModelNew`. Potential issue: The CUDA code uses `at::Tensor` and `AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES`. We need to include `<torch/extension.h>` and `<ATen/ATen.h>`. But as discussed, we can put declaration in `cpp_sources` and definition in `cuda_sources`. Let's draft: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> at::Tensor depthwise_conv2d_k1_forward( const at::Tensor& input, const at::Tensor& weight, const at::Tensor& bias, int64_t stride, int64_t padding, int64_t dilation, bool bias_flag); """ cuda_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <ATen/cuda/CUDAContext.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void depthwise_conv2d_k1_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation, bool bias_flag) { int64_t idx = static_cast<int64_t>(blockIdx.x) * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int64_t total = static_cast<int64_t>(N) * C * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; int64_t HW_out = static_cast<int64_t>(H_out) * W_out; int64_t CHW_out = static_cast<int64_t>(C) * HW_out; int n = static_cast<int>(idx / CHW_out); int64_t rem = idx % CHW_out; int c = static_cast<int>(rem / HW_out); rem = rem % HW_out; int h = static_cast<int>(rem / W_out); int w = static_cast<int>(rem % W_out); scalar_t acc = bias_flag ? bias[c] : static_cast<scalar_t>(0); int w_in = w * stride - padding; if (w_in >= 0 && w_in < W) { int nch_offset = (n * C + c) * H; int h_in_start = h * stride - padding; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_curr = h_in_start + kh * dilation; if (h_curr >= 0 && h_curr < H) { scalar_t val = input[(nch_offset + h_curr) * W + w_in]; scalar_t wgt = weight[c * K + kh]; acc += val * wgt; } } } output[idx] = acc; } at::Tensor depthwise_conv2d_k1_forward( const at::Tensor& input, const at::Tensor& weight, const at::Tensor& bias, int64_t stride, int64_t padding, int64_t dilation, bool bias_flag) { int N = input.size(0); int C = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int K = weight.size(2); int H_out = (H + 2 * padding - dilation * (K - 1) - 1) / static_cast<int>(stride) + 1; int W_out = (W + 2 * padding - 1) / static_cast<int>(stride) + 1; auto output = at::empty({N, C, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); int64_t total = static_cast<int64_t>(N) * C * H_out * W_out; const int block_size = 256; int grid_size = static_cast<int>((total + block_size - 1) / block_size); AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(input.scalar_type(), "depthwise_conv2d_k1", [&] { const scalar_t* input_ptr = input.data_ptr<scalar_t>(); const scalar_t* weight_ptr = weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(); const scalar_t* bias_ptr = bias_flag ? bias.data_ptr<scalar_t>() : nullptr; scalar_t* output_ptr = output.data_ptr<scalar_t>(); depthwise_conv2d_k1_kernel<scalar_t><<<grid_size, block_size, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( input_ptr, weight_ptr, bias_ptr, output_ptr, N, C, H, W, H_out, W_out, K, static_cast<int>(stride), static_cast<int>(padding), static_cast<int>(dilation), bias_flag ); }); return output; } """ _depthwise_conv2d_k1 = load_inline( name="depthwise_conv2d_k1", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["depthwise_conv2d_k1_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1, bias=False): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.conv2d = nn.Conv2d( in_channels, in_channels, kernel_size=(kernel_size, 1), stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, groups=in_channels, bias=bias ) def forward(self, x): bias = self.conv2d.bias return _depthwise_conv2d_k1.depthwise_conv2d_k1_forward( x.contiguous(), self.conv2d.weight.contiguous(), bias.contiguous() if bias is not None else x.new_empty(1), self.conv2d.stride[0] if isinstance(self.conv2d.stride, tuple) else self.conv2d.stride, self.conv2d.padding[0] if isinstance(self.conv2d.padding, tuple) else self.conv2d.padding, self.conv2d.dilation[0] if isinstance(self.conv2d.dilation, tuple) else self.conv2d.dilation, bias is not None ) ``` Wait, `self.conv2d.stride` is a tuple of two ints (or a single int converted to tuple). Let me implement this: ```cuda template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void depthwise_conv2d_k1_kernel_v2( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation, bool bias_flag) { // Each thread handles one (n, c, w) and all h values int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total_ncw = N * C * W_out; if (tid >= total_ncw) return; int nc = tid / W_out; int w = tid % W_out; int n = nc / C; int c = nc % C; int w_in = w * stride - padding; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) { // All outputs for this (n,c,w) are zero/padding for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { output[((n * C + c) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = bias_flag ? bias[c] : 0; } return; } // Base pointer for this (n, c, w_in) const scalar_t* input_base = input + ((n * C + c) * H + 0) * W + w_in; scalar_t* output_base = output + ((n * C + c) * H_out + 0) * W_out + w; scalar_t wgt[8]; // Support up to K=8 for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { wgt[kh] = weight[c * K + kh]; } scalar_t b = bias_flag ? bias[c] : 0; for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { scalar_t acc = b; int h_in_start = h * stride - padding; for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_curr = h_in_start + kh * dilation; if (h_curr >= 0 && h_curr < H) { acc += input_base[h_curr * W] * wgt[kh]; } } output_base[h * W_out] = acc; } } ``` Hmm, the boundary check inside the inner loop is problematic. When h reaches H_out-1, we're trying to read input_col[(H_out-1 + K) * W] = input_col[H * W], which is out of bounds! For h = H_out - 1 = H - K (when stride=1, dilation=1, padding=0): - We need to read h+K = H-K+K = H, which is exactly 1 past the last row. - But we only need this read AFTER computing the output, and then the loop ends. - So we should restructure: read K values, compute output 0, read value K, compute output 1, ..., read value H-1, compute output H-K. Let me fix this: ```cuda // Sliding window optimization scalar_t window[8]; // K max = 7 or so int h_in = 0; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { window[kh] = input_col[h_in * W]; h_in++; } for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { scalar_t acc = b; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { acc += window[kh] * weight[c * K + kh]; } output_col[h * W_out] = acc; // Slide window: shift and load new value #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K - 1; ++kh) { window[kh] = window[kh + 1]; } if (h < H_out - 1) { window[K - 1] = input_col[h_in * W]; h_in++; } } ``` This looks better. But the branch `if (h < H_out - 1)` might cause divergence for the last iteration. Since it's just one iteration out of H_out, the divergence is minimal. We could also use a sentinel or just do the read (it might read slightly out of bounds but we don't use it). Actually reading out of bounds is undefined behavior, so better to avoid it. Actually, let me think about whether the sliding window is even worth it. Without it, each output reads K input values, for a total of K*H_out reads per column. With the sliding window, we read H + K - 1 values per column, which is strictly less than K*H_out for K > 1 and H_out > 1. For K=3, H=512, H_out=510: - Without sliding window: 3 * 510 = 1530 reads - With sliding window: 512 + 2 = 514 reads - That's a 3x reduction in input memory reads! And since input memory bandwidth is the bottleneck, this should give a significant speedup. However, with register pressure, K values per thread might increase register usage. For K=3, it's 3 registers, which is fine. Let me also consider using `__ldg` for input reads to go through read-only texture cache, which might help bandwidth. For the general case (padding > 0, or when boundary checks are needed), I'll use a separate kernel. Let me also think about vectorization. For the sliding window, each thread reads one float at a time. The loads aren't vectorized, but they are contiguous for adjacent h values. Actually, within a thread, loads are separated by W * sizeof(float), which for W=512 is 2048 bytes = 32 cache lines (assuming 64-byte cache lines). These are strided loads and won't benefit from cache line locality for consecutive loads in time. Wait, but we're reading the same column, so each read is W floats apart in memory. This means each read loads a new cache line. So each input read is essentially a cold cache miss! Oh wait, that's an important observation. In the column-major approach, each thread reads down a column. Since W=512, consecutive reads in the same column are 512*4=2048 bytes apart. This means the stride is 32 cache lines (assuming 64B cache lines). So each read causes a cache miss and loads exactly one float (wasting 63 bytes of the cache line). This is TERRIBLE for cache efficiency! In contrast, the original element-wise kernel where each warp processes adjacent (n,c,h,w) values has: - Adjacent threads in a warp read adjacent w values at the same h - So they read consecutive memory locations - This is fully coalesced and makes good use of cache lines So my "column processing per thread" idea might actually be WORSE than the original element-wise approach due to poor spatial locality in the column direction! Wait, actually let me reconsider. In the original kernel: - Thread 0 reads input at (n, c, h, w) - Thread 1 reads input at (n, c, h, w+1) - These are adjacent in memory, so coalesced - But for K=3, each thread reads 3 values: (n,c,h,w), (n,c,h+1,w), (n,c,h+2,w) - These are strided by W in memory - So each thread causes 3 cache misses per output In the column kernel: - Thread 0 reads all values for (n,c,*,w) sequentially - Each read is strided by W - But if multiple threads in a warp process different w values at the same h, they still coalesce for the first read of each output Actually, I think the original element-wise approach with all threads in a warp working on the same (n,c,h) but different w is better for memory coalescing. But it reads each input value K times. The sliding window approach in a column reduces total reads by Kx but has poor cache locality for individual reads. Hmm, let me think about whether GPU caches can help. If warp size is 32, and 32 threads process w=0,1,2,...31 at the same h: - For kh=0: threads read (n,c,h,0..31) - fully coalesced, 4 cache lines - For kh=1: threads read (n,c,h+1,0..31) - fully coalesced, 4 cache lines - For kh=2: threads read (n,c,h+2,0..31) - fully coalesced, 4 cache lines So each warp reads 12 cache lines to compute 32 outputs. Total memory: 32*3*4 = 384 bytes, loaded via 12 cache lines = 768 bytes. Some waste, but not terrible. For H=512, W=512, the total L2 cache could hold several rows. If we're reading rows h, h+1, h+2 for many w values, the rows might stay in L2. The column approach: - Thread 0 reads (n,c,0,w), (n,c,1,w), ..., (n,c,K-1,w) for output 0 - Then (n,c,1,w), (n,c,2,w), ..., (n,c,K,w) for output 1 - Each read is one cache line but only uses 4 bytes - Total cache lines read: H + K - 1 per column - Total bytes loaded from DRAM: ~(H + K) * 64 bytes (since each cache line is 64 bytes and only 4 bytes used) Wait, that doesn't make sense. The GPU L2 cache line is 32 bytes, not 64. Even so, we're wasting 28 bytes per load. For H=512, K=3: - Column approach: 514 cache lines * 32 bytes = ~16KB per column - Total for all columns: 261K columns * 16KB = way more than DRAM bandwidth Actually no, cache lines are loaded once and can be reused by other threads. If thread 0 loads (n,c,0,w), it brings the cache line containing (n,c,0,w..w+7) into L2. If another thread is processing w+1, it might hit in L2. But in the column approach, each thread processes a single w, so other threads in the same warp process different w values. When they all do their first load at h=0, they load adjacent cache lines which is coalesced. Then when they proceed to h=1, they load cache lines for row 1, which are also adjacent and coalesced. But the key difference is: in the element-wise approach, a warp processes 32 adjacent outputs at once, reading 3 rows * 32 columns. In the column approach, a warp processes 32 different columns, computing one output per column, reading 1 row * 32 columns. Then the next step reads the next row * 32 columns. Actually, both approaches have similar memory access patterns for cache lines, just at different granularities. In the element-wise approach, a warp touches 3 rows x 32 columns. In the column approach, a warp touches 1 row x 32 columns per step, doing K steps. The total number of cache line loads is similar. But with the sliding window in column approach, we only load each row once per column instead of K times per column. Wait, actually in the element-wise approach with 32 threads, each input at (n,c,h_in,w) is read by K different warps (for K different output h values). In the column approach, it's read once per column. So the column approach with sliding window IS better for reducing total memory traffic. But the issue is that within a single warp, 32 threads are accessing 32 different columns. When they collectively read all w values for a given h_in, that's still coalesced. And when they move to h_in+1, they read the next row, also coalesced. The concern was whether L2 cache can hold all the relevant rows. For H=512, each row for one (n,c) is 512*4 = 2KB. With N=64, C=8, total rows = 512. L2 cache on modern GPUs is 4-6MB, which could hold ~1000 rows of 2KB. So all rows for a few (n,c) pairs can fit in L2. Actually, I think the column approach with sliding window should work well, especially with the reduced total memory reads. Wait, but in the column approach, after computing output h=0, we move to h=1. The cache lines for h=0 might be evicted before they're reused. If a warp computes a tile of H x W outputs, it can load a tile of input into shared memory and reuse it. Let me think about shared memory tiling: - A block handles a tile of TH x TW outputs - To compute these outputs, it needs (TH + K - 1) x TW input pixels (with stride=1, dilation=1) - Load this input tile into shared memory - Each thread computes one or more outputs from the shared memory tile For K=3: - If TH=16, TW=32, input tile = 18 x 32 = 576 floats = 2.3KB - This easily fits in shared memory - Each input pixel is read once from DRAM and reused by up to K=3 threads This is the classic convolution tiling approach! And the memory access pattern is great: - Loading the input tile: coalesced (each thread loads a float4 or just consecutive floats) - Computing: shared memory reads are fast and bank-conflict-free if laid out correctly This should be a big win! Let me implement this. Shared memory tiling for 2D conv: - Block size: 16x16 threads or 16x32 threads - Tile size: same as block size for outputs - Input tile: (TH + K - 1) x (TW) With the kernel being (K, 1), it's actually 1D along height, so the width dimension doesn't need padding in the input tile. For kernel (K, 1): - Output tile: TH x TW - Input tile: (TH + K - 1) x TW Each thread in the block loads one or more input pixels into shared memory: - Total input pixels: (TH + K - 1) * TW - Threads: TH * TW (if block = output tile) - So some threads load 2 input pixels, or we can use a different block size Let me use: - Block: 16 threads in H, 16 threads in W = 256 threads - Output tile: 16 x 16 - Input tile: (16 + K - 1) x 16 = (17 to 23) x 16 = 272 to 368 floats - Each of 256 threads loads 1-2 floats The loading can be done with: ```cuda for (int i = tid; i < input_tile_size; i += block_size) { int ih = i / TW; int iw = i % TW; // load input[n, c, h_base + ih, w_base + iw] into smem[ih][iw] } ``` After loading, use `__syncthreads()`. Then each thread computes its output: ```cuda int h = threadIdx.y; int w = threadIdx.x; scalar_t acc = bias[c]; for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { acc += smem[h + kh][w] * weight[c * K + kh]; } output[n, c, h_base + h, w_base + w] = acc; ``` This avoids redundant DRAM reads and should be much faster! But there's a problem: the block tiles across H and W, but also needs to iterate over N and C. We can let grid.x handle W, grid.y handle H, and each block iterates over N and C. Alternatively, let each block handle one (n, c) and a tile of (H, W). This means: - grid.x = ceil(W_out / TW) - grid.y = ceil(H_out / TH) - grid.z = N * C For N=64, C=8, H_out=510, W_out=510, TH=16, TW=16: - grid = (32, 32, 512) - Total blocks = 524288 That's a lot of blocks, but each block is small (256 threads). The GPU should handle it. Actually, maybe I should also tile over C to reduce the number of blocks and increase per-block work. But with N*C=512, it's already fine. Wait, another constraint: shared memory size. With K=3: - Input tile = 18 * 16 * 4 bytes = 1.15KB per block - Very small, we can easily have many blocks resident per SM Occupancy would be very high since shared memory usage is tiny. Let me also think about how to handle padding. The input tile includes the padding region, so we need to fill with zeros when loading. For the fast path (padding=0), we don't need this. For general padding, during loading: - If the source pixel is out of bounds, store 0 in shared memory - This requires boundary checks during loading For the test case (padding=0), the boundary check can be skipped. Additionally, since the kernel doesn't tile along the width dimension (K_w=1), we only need to extend the input tile in the height direction. This keeps the shared memory small. For padding > 0, add boundary checks during loading Cuda code sketch: ```cuda template <typename scalar_t, int TH, int TW, int K_MAX> __global__ void depthwise_conv2d_k1_tiled_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int stride_h, int stride_w, int padding_h, int padding_w, int dilation_h, int dilation_w, bool bias_flag) { // Block dimensions: (TW, TH) // Grid: (ceil(W_out/TW), ceil(H_out/TH), N*C) const int w_tid = threadIdx.x; const int h_tid = threadIdx.y; const int tid = h_tid * blockDim.x + w_tid; const int b_w = blockIdx.x * TW; const int b_h = blockIdx.y * TH; const int nc = blockIdx.z; const int n = nc / C; const int c = nc % C; // Input tile size in shared memory // For kernel (K, 1), we only extend in H direction const int INPUT_H = TH * stride_h + (K - 1) * dilation_h; const int INPUT_W = TW * stride_w; // since kernel width is 1 __shared__ scalar_t smem[INPUT_H][INPUT_W]; // Load input tile into shared memory // Each thread loads multiple pixels if needed const int input_tile_size = INPUT_H * INPUT_W; #pragma unroll for (int i = tid; i < input_tile_size; i += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int ih = i / INPUT_W; int iw = i % INPUT_W; int h_in = b_h * stride_h - padding_h + ih * dilation_h; int w_in = b_w * stride_w - padding_w + iw * stride_w; // For kernel width=1, with stride_w and padding_w: // Actually wait, w dimension doesn't have kernel, so: // Output w maps to input w_in = w * stride_w - padding_w // So for tile starting at b_w, w_in for first column = b_w * stride_w - padding_w // For iw offset within tile: w_in = b_w * stride_w - padding_w + iw // But iw should range from 0 to TW-1, and each column in the tile // corresponds to one output column. So: int actual_w_in = b_w * stride_w - padding_w + iw; if (h_in >= 0 && h_in < H && actual_w_in >= 0 && actual_w_in < W) { smem[ih][iw] = input[((n * C + c) * H + h_in) * W + actual_w_in]; } else { smem[ih][iw] = 0; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute output for this thread int h_out = b_h + h_tid; int w_out = b_w + w_tid; if (h_out < H_out && w_out < W_out) { scalar_t acc = bias_flag ? bias[c] : 0; int h_in_start = h_out * stride_h - padding_h; // In shared memory, the relevant input starts at: // h_in_start - (b_h * stride_h - padding_h) = (h_out - b_h) * stride_h = h_tid * stride_h int smem_h_start = h_tid * stride_h; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int smem_h = smem_h_start + kh * dilation_h; scalar_t val = smem[smem_h][w_tid]; scalar_t wgt = weight[c * K + kh]; acc += val * wgt; } output[((n * C + c) * H_out + h_out) * W_out + w_out] = acc; } } ``` Hmm wait, I made an error in computing the shared memory layout. We can use a register-blocking approach where each thread computes a small tile of outputs and keeps a sliding window of input values. Let me try this simpler approach: ```cuda template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void depthwise_conv2d_k1_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation, bool bias_flag) { // Process 4 adjacent w values per thread // Each thread computes all H outputs for w, w+1, w+2, w+3 const int w_per_thread = 4; int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total_w_groups = N * C * ((W_out + w_per_thread - 1) / w_per_thread); if (tid >= total_w_groups) return; int w_group = tid % ((W_out + w_per_thread - 1) / w_per_thread); int nc = tid / ((W_out + w_per_thread - 1) / w_per_thread); int n = nc / C; int c = nc % C; scalar_t wgt[8]; for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) wgt[kh] = weight[c * K + kh]; scalar_t b = bias_flag ? bias[c] : 0; #pragma unroll for (int dw = 0; dw < w_per_thread; ++dw) { int w = w_group * w_per_thread + dw; if (w >= W_out) continue; int w_in = w * stride - padding; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) { #pragma unroll for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { output[((n * C + c) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = b; } continue; } // Fast path for padding=0 along H // Precompute valid h range int h_valid_start = 0; int h_valid_end = H_out; // If padding > 0, adjust boundaries // ... // Top edge for (int h = 0; h < h_valid_start; ++h) { // ... } // Interior: no boundary checks for (int h = h_valid_start; h < h_valid_end; ++h) { scalar_t acc = b; int h_in_start = h * stride - padding; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_curr = h_in_start + kh * dilation; acc += input[((n * C + c) * H + h_curr) * W + w_in] * wgt[kh]; } output[((n * C + c) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = acc; } // Bottom edge for (int h = h_valid_end; h < H_out; ++h) { // ... } } } ``` Hmm, this is getting complex. And the W_out dimension might not be divisible by 4, causing some threads to do less work. Let me try the simplest approach that might still work: per-thread column processing with no inter-thread cooperation, but with the sliding window register optimization for the common case. Wait, I just realized something. In my very first kernel, the memory access pattern for input is: - Thread i: reads input at (n, c, h + kh, w) - Adjacent threads read adjacent w values For the weight, all threads for the same c read the same weight values. This means weight can be cached in L1, but since threads for different c are in the same warp (because c is determined by idx % CHW_out / HW_out), adjacent threads might have different c values, causing all threads in the warp to access different weights. Let me check: for N=64, C=8, H_out=510, W_out=510: - CHW_out = 8 * 510 * 510 = 2,080,800 - HW_out = 510 * 510 = 260,100 - idx / CHW_out = n - (idx % CHW_out) / HW_out = c - So adjacent threads with idx differing by 1 have the same n but might have different c if they cross an HW_out boundary. For a warp of 32 threads, if thread i has c = floor((i % CHW_out) / HW_out), then for i in [0, 31], c is the same (since HW_out >> 32). So all threads in a warp have the same c! That's great for weight caching. Wait, actually for general idx, a warp covers 32 consecutive idx values. If 32 < HW_out, then all 32 threads in a warp have the same n and c, and differ only in h and w. This is almost always true since HW_out is large. So in my original kernel, all threads in a warp share the same n and c. They differ in h and w. The weight accesses are uniform (all threads access the same weight values), so they broadcast from L1. The input accesses are for different (h, w) but same (n, c). For kh=0, they access the same h but different w - fully coalesced. For kh=1, same h+1 but different w - also coalesced. This memory access pattern is actually quite good! The main issue is that each input pixel is read K times (once for each kh where it appears in the receptive field). For K=3, each interior input pixel is read 3 times. For memory bandwidth, this means we're reading 3x the necessary data from DRAM (modulo cache hits). If the data fits in L2 cache, subsequent reads might hit. For 64*8*512*512*4 bytes = 512MB input, L2 cache is ~4-6MB, so we can hold ~1% of the input. But each row for one (n,c) is 2KB. If we process all outputs for one (n,c) sequentially, the rows might stay in cache. But in the original kernel, the grid processes all (n,c,h,w) in parallel. There's no spatial locality for caching because threads are randomly distributed across the entire input. If I use a column-based approach where a warp processes columns for one (n,c), I could get better locality. But then I'd lose the coalescing within a warp. Trade-off: better cache locality (fewer total DRAM reads) vs. better coalescing (more efficient per-read bandwidth). For the element-wise kernel: - Total input reads = output_elements * K * reads_per_input = 133M * 3 = 400M - With coalescing: each warp reads 32 floats in one transaction - So ~400M/32 = 12.5M warp transactions for input - Plus 133M output writes - Total memory bandwidth ~ (400M * 4 + 133M * 4) bytes / time For the column kernel with sliding window: - Total input reads = N * C * W * H = input_size = 68M (once per input element) - But with strided access, each read is a separate cache line (wasted bandwidth) - Total cache lines: ~68M - Assuming 32-byte cache lines: ~68M * 32 bytes = ~2.2GB of cache traffic - Wait, but each warp of 32 threads each reading one float from different cache lines... no, adjacent threads read adjacent w values, so they ARE coalesced. They read 32 floats that are spaced by H*W? NO. In the column kernel, thread 0 reads column w=0, thread 1 reads column w=1. When they all read row h=0, they're reading input[n,c,0,0], input[n,c,0,1], etc. These ARE adjacent and coalesced! When they move to h=1, they read input[n,c,1,0], input[n,c,1,1], etc. Also adjacent and coalesced. So the column kernel ALSO has fully coalesced reads! And with the sliding window, each input value is read only once. For K=3, total input reads: - Element-wise: ~400M - Column sliding window: ~68M (the entire input once) That's a 6x reduction in input reads! The output writes are the same. But wait, the element-wise kernel's reads might hit in cache because of reuse. Let me think about this. In the element-wise kernel, thread idx computes output at some (n,c,h,w). For kh=0, it reads input[n,c,h,w]. For kh=1, it reads input[n,c,h+1,w]. For kh=2, input[n,c,h+2,w]. Another thread idx+W_out computes output at (n,c,h+1,w). It reads input[n,c,h+1,w], input[n,c,h+2,w], input[n,c,h+3,w]. So input[n,c,h+2,w] is read by both threads (for kh=2 of first, kh=1 of second). If these threads run close in time and the cache line is still resident, the second read might hit in L1/L2. But in the original kernel, threads are scattered across the entire grid. Thread idx and idx+W_out might be in different warps that run at different times. In a column kernel, a warp processes different w values but same h. So when it moves from h to h+1, the previous row might still be in L1 if the warp processes quickly enough. For the fast path (padding=0), here's my improved kernel: ```cuda template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void depthwise_conv2d_k1_fast_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int stride, int dilation, bool bias_flag) { int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * W_out; if (tid >= total) return; int nc = tid / W_out; int w = tid % W_out; int n = nc / C; int c = nc % C; const scalar_t* input_base = input + ((n * C + c) * H) * W; scalar_t* output_base = output + ((n * C + c) * H_out) * W_out + w; scalar_t wgt[8]; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { wgt[kh] = weight[c * K + kh]; } scalar_t b = bias_flag ? bias[c] : 0; int w_in = w * stride; // padding = 0 if (stride == 1 && dilation == 1) { // Sliding window: optimal for stride=1, dilation=1 scalar_t window[8]; int pos = 0; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { window[kh] = input_base[pos * W + w_in]; pos++; } for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { scalar_t acc = b; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { acc += window[kh] * wgt[kh]; } output_base[h * W_out] = acc; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K - 1; ++kh) { window[kh] = window[kh + 1]; } if (h < H_out - 1) { window[K - 1] = input_base[pos * W + w_in]; pos++; } } } else { // General case without boundary checks (padding=0) for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { scalar_t acc = b; int h_in_start = h * stride; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_curr = h_in_start + kh * dilation; acc += input_base[h_curr * W + w_in] * wgt[kh]; } output_base[h * W_out] = acc; } } } ``` This looks clean. Actually, I can write one kernel that handles everything, with conditional compilation inside: ```cuda template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void depthwise_conv2d_k1_kernel_v2( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation, bool bias_flag) { int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * W_out; if (tid >= total) return; int nc = tid / W_out; int w = tid % W_out; int n = nc / C; int c = nc % C; const scalar_t* input_nc = input + ((n * C + c) * H) * W; scalar_t* output_ncw = output + ((n * C + c) * H_out) * W_out + w; scalar_t wgt[8]; #pragma unroll 8 for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { wgt[kh] = weight[c * K + kh]; } scalar_t b = bias_flag ? bias[c] : 0; int w_in = w * stride - padding; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) { // w is entirely out of bounds: fill with bias for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { output_ncw[h * W_out] = b; } return; } if (padding == 0 && stride == 1 && dilation == 1) { // Fast sliding window path scalar_t window[8]; int read_pos = 0; #pragma unroll 8 for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { window[kh] = input_nc[read_pos * W + w_in]; read_pos++; } for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { scalar_t acc = b; #pragma unroll 8 for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { acc += window[kh] * wgt[kh]; } output_ncw[h * W_out] = acc; #pragma unroll 8 for (int kh = 0; kh < K - 1; ++kh) { window[kh] = window[kh + 1]; } if (h < H_out - 1) { window[K - 1] = input_nc[read_pos * W + w_in]; read_pos++; } } } else { // General path for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { scalar_t acc = b; int h_in_start = h * stride - padding; #pragma unroll 8 for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_curr = h_in_start + kh * dilation; if (h_curr >= 0 && h_curr < H) { acc += input_nc[h_curr * W + w_in] * wgt[kh]; } } output_ncw[h * W_out] = acc; } } } ``` This is clean. I'll include both a fast path and a general path. For the general path, I can also do some optimization: precompute the valid h range to avoid boundary checks in the inner loop. For a given (n, c, w): - h_in_start = h * stride - padding - For h_in_start to be valid (>= 0): h >= ceil(padding / stride) - For h_in_start + (K-1)*dilation to be valid (< H): h < floor((H - 1 + padding - (K-1)*dilation) / stride) + 1 Actually, let me just compute the valid h range in the kernel: ```cuda int h_min = 0; while (h_min < H_out && h_min * stride - padding < 0) h_min++; int h_max = H_out; while (h_max > 0 && (h_max - 1) * stride - padding + (K - 1) * dilation >= H) h_max--; ``` But `while` loops are expensive. Better to compute directly: ```cuda int h_min = (padding + stride - 1) / stride; // ceil(padding / stride) if (h_min < 0) h_min = 0; int h_max = (H + padding - (K - 1) * dilation) / stride; if (h_max > H_out) h_max = H_out; ``` For padding >= 0, stride = 1, dilation = 1: - h_min = padding - h_max = H + padding - K + 1 = H - K + 1 + padding = H_out + padding - padding = H_out? Wait. - H_out = H + 2*padding - K + 1 (for stride=1, dilation=1) - h_max = (H + padding - K + 1) / 1 = H + padding - K + 1 - For padding > 0, this is > H_out? No, H_out = H + 2*padding - K + 1, so h_max = H_out - padding. - Hmm, that's not right either. Let me recalculate. The condition for h to be fully interior (all K pixels in bounds): - h * stride - padding + kh * dilation >= 0 for all kh in [0, K-1] => h * stride - padding >= 0 => h >= padding / stride (for stride=1: h >= padding) - h * stride - padding + (K-1) * dilation < H for all h => h * stride < H + padding - (K-1) * dilation => h < (H + padding - (K-1) * dilation) / stride So valid h range for all pixels in bounds: - h_min = ceil(padding / stride) - h_max = floor((H + padding - (K-1) * dilation - 1) / stride) + 1? No, strict inequality h * stride < H + padding - (K-1)*dilation. - h < (H + padding - (K-1) * dilation) / stride For padding=0, stride=1, dilation=1, K=3: - h_min = 0 - h_max = (H - 2) / 1 = H - 2 - H_out = H - 3 + 1 = H - 2 - So h_max = H_out. All h values are valid. Correct! For padding=1, stride=1, dilation=1, K=3: - h_min = 1 - h_max = (H + 1 - 2) / 1 = H - 1 - H_out = H + 2 - 3 + 1 = H - So h in [1, H-1) = [1, H-2] is fully valid. h=0 and h=H-1=H_out-1 need boundary checks. - Total valid: H-2 values out of H. Good. So the formula works. I'll use this in the kernel to skip boundary checks for the interior region. But for now, let me focus on the fast path which covers the test case. Alright, enough analysis. Let me write the code! I'll write a kernel with: 1. Fast path for padding=0, stride=1, dilation=1 with sliding window 2. Medium path for padding=0 (no boundary checks on input) 3. Safe path for general case Actually, I'll combine 1 and 2: when padding=0, there are no boundary checks needed at all. When additionally stride=1 and dilation=1, use sliding window. So: - If padding == 0 && stride == 1 && dilation == 1: sliding window, no checks - Else if padding == 0: no h boundary checks, but may have strided access - Else: general with boundary checks Let me finalize the code.

reasoning
The v2 kernel also passed. Let's see how fast it is with a quick timing. I'll write a small timing script and run it directly - but I don't have a specific timing tool other than profile_kernel, which isn't working. Actually, I can now move further with optimizations. Let's understand the problem better: - The kernel is depthwise conv2d with a (K, 1) kernel. - The reference uses nn.Conv2d which calls cuDNN. - For small channels (8) and small kernel (3x1), cuDNN might not be perfectly optimized. Looking at v2 kernel: - One thread computes all H_out positions for a single (n, c, w) coordinate. - This gives excellent locality and avoids redundant reads. - For padding=0, stride=1, dilation=1 case with kernel_size=3, we reuse K-1 of K pixels between adjacent outputs via sliding window. Let me think about further optimizations: 1. Use const window size for kernel size 3 since that's the test case. Actually, for maximum performance with this type of convolution, I should maybe try to use shared memory or vectorized loads. Wait - since K=3, each thread computes many outputs using a sliding window. The arithmetic intensity is: - For H_out positions, we read K + H_out - 1 input values, H_out * K multiply-adds. - For large H=512, H_out=510, we read 512 values and do 1530 MADDs. That's ~3 FLOPs per byte read. With memory bandwidth of ~900GB/s, peak would be ~675 GFLOPs, which is well below compute peak. So this is memory bandwidth bound. - The key is maximizing memory bandwidth utilization. Can we do vectorized loads? e.g., load float4 at a time? Since W=512 and the access pattern is along H dimension with stride W: `input_nc + h*W + w_in`. If w_in is fixed per thread, the stride between consecutive H accesses is W=512*sizeof(float)=2KB. This is not consecutive memory access. It's strided. Actually, this means each thread series is striding by 2KB. If multiple threads in a warp write to adjacent W's, they will access input simultaneously for the same H but different W's. E.g., W_out threads with W_in=0..W_out-1, accessing H=0. This is a coalesced access across the width! Each thread reads one float, total for warp is 32 consecutive floats = 128 bytes. That's perfectly coalesced. So the memory access pattern is good across threads (coalesced for same H, different W), but as said before, consecutive H values for the same thread are far apart (2KB), but as long as they are cached (they are, no capacity conflicts since arrays are 2MB, sets = 2MB/2KB =... maybe some conflict, but H is 512, which is 2^9, if cache has 2^8 sets, this conflicts!), there might be cache line conflicts! With L1 128KB shared, H=512, each column at one H h and all W consumes 512*4=2048 bytes. So total 512*2KB = 1MB. The cache has 128KB and is 4-way or similar. This means reuse is bad across H because it won't all fit in cache. But sliding window helps because the same cache lines from previous reads may still be there. Let's see: - For a single thread processing all H_out=510 positions, it reads 512 H values (for W_in fixed). Total is 512*4=2KB. This easily fits in L1 cache. - But this is for ONE thread. For a warp (32 threads) processing 32 different W_in: - For H=0, they read 32 consecutive floats = 1 cache line. Coalesced. - The cache line read contains 128 bytes = 32 floats. - After reading H=0, H=1 for the same warp requires again 1 cache line offset by 2KB. - Total cache lines for the warp for H=0 and H=1 are 2 lines in different sets. - If W=512 is aligned to 128 bytes (it is probably aligned to say 256 bytes), the address pattern could conflict with L1 cache sets! Actually, let's think about set-associative cache. L1 cache on Ampere is 128 KB, 4-way or 8-way, 128B lines. Number of sets = 128KB / 128B / ways. Assuming 4-way: 128KB/(128B*4) = 256 sets. Address A maps to set (A/128) % 256. For h*W + w_in: if W=512, h=0, w_in=0 -> line 0 h=1, w_in=0 -> line 512 % 256 = 0. SAME SET. h=2, w_in=0 -> line 1024 % 256 = 0. SAME SET. So all H values for the same W_in map to the SAME cache set! With 4-way associativity, only 4 lines from this sequence can be in cache at once. That means out of K=3 or H=512 reads, we can only cache 4 values along H (for the same W_in). But H=512 >> 4, so almost all reads are cache misses! This is bad. For 32 threads in a warp: w_in=0..31 map to lines 0..3 (since 32*4 = 128B = 1 line). So w_in=0..31 all compete for 1 line (same set? line 0 for h=0). Wait, if W=512 and input is contiguous, then address of (h, w) = base + (h * 512 + w) * 4. Cache line index = floor((base + (h*512 + w)*4)/128) % Sets. For base aligned to say 256: base/128 is even. ((h*512 + w)/32) % 256. For w=0: (h*512/32) % 256 = (h*16) % 256. So for h=0: 0%256 = 0 h=1: 16%256 = 16 h=2: 32%256 = 32 ... h=15: 240%256 = 240 h=16: 256%256 = 0 -> same set as h=0. So for same W, heights 0 and 16 share a set. 4-way means heights 0,16,32,48 can be cached. That covers a window of 49, which is > K=3. For general use, this means K won't conflict. But for H=512, height 0..15 use distinct sets (0,16,32,...,240). Then height 16 overlaps with h=0. So for K=3, no conflict issue. For the warp: different W values in the same line (w=0..31 for h=0) all map to set 0. So set 0 has line from (h=0,w=0..31), and also from other h where h*16 % 256 == 0, i.e., h multiples of 16. So at h=0 and h=16, the line covers w=0..31 for both. But in our sliding window, we only keep K=3 lines. K=3 < 16, so no conflict. So the cache should actually handle K=3 well. But if h values are far apart (e.g., for padded or strided), they might conflict if separated by 16. Wait, what about multiple warps in the same block? If threads in the same block process adjacent C or N, they might read completely different memory and not interfere with the cache lines this warp is using. Or they could share the same W_in and thus the same lines, which is good. Actually, the primary issue with cache conflicts would be if W_in values cause lines to be displaced. But since W_in is constant for one thread, and H values are spaced by 16 in set index, K=3 values won't conflict. So memory bandwidth should be well-utilized.
