So the fast path works for all W. For the general case with padding, W_out could be smaller. Here's v3: ```cpp template <typename scalar_t> __launch_bounds__(256) __global__ void depthwise_conv2d_k1_kernel_fast( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, bool bias_flag) { // Assumes K=3, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1 int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * W_out; if (tid >= total) return; int nc = tid / W_out; int w = tid % W_out; int n = nc / C; int c = nc % C; const scalar_t* input_nc = input + ((n * C + c) * H + 0) * W + w; scalar_t* output_ncw = output + ((n * C + c) * H_out + 0) * W_out + w; scalar_t w0 = weight[c * 3 + 0]; scalar_t w1 = weight[c * 3 + 1]; scalar_t w2 = weight[c * 3 + 2]; scalar_t b = bias_flag ? bias[c] : static_cast<scalar_t>(0); scalar_t v0 = __ldg(input_nc + 0 * W); scalar_t v1 = __ldg(input_nc + 1 * W); scalar_t v2 = __ldg(input_nc + 2 * W); #pragma unroll 8 for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { output_ncw[h * W_out] = b + v0 * w0 + v1 * w1 + v2 * w2; v0 = v1; v1 = v2; if (h + 3 < H) v2 = __ldg(input_nc + (h + 3) * W); } } ``` This is much cleaner. The pointer arithmetic is simplified: `input_nc` points to column `w` of the first row. Then `input_nc + h*W` is the element at row h, column w. This should compile to very efficient code: - `v0 = v1; v1 = v2;` are register moves. - The load `v2 = __ldg(input_nc + (h+3)*W)` - the compiler may strength-reduce the address computation by incrementing a pointer by W each iteration. - The store `output_ncw[h*W_out]` - since W_out is a runtime parameter, the compiler might not strength-reduce it. But it could use a running pointer: `output_ptr += W_out` each iteration. Actually, I can explicitly use a running pointer to help the compiler: ```cpp scalar_t* out_ptr = output_ncw; #pragma unroll 8 for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { *out_ptr = b + v0*w0 + v1*w1 + v2*w2; out_ptr += W_out; v0 = v1; v1 = v2; if (h + 3 < H) v2 = __ldg(in_ptr + (h+3)*W); } ``` Wait, `in_ptr = input_nc` is fixed. So `in_ptr + (h+3)*W`. We can also maintain an input pointer: ```cpp const scalar_t* in_ptr = input_nc; scalar_t v0 = __ldg(in_ptr); in_ptr += W; scalar_t v1 = __ldg(in_ptr); in_ptr += W; scalar_t v2 = __ldg(in_ptr); in_ptr += W; #pragma unroll 8 for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { *out_ptr = b + v0*w0 + v1*w1 + v2*w2; out_ptr += W_out; v0 = v1; v1 = v2; if (h + 3 < H) { v2 = __ldg(in_ptr); in_ptr += W; } } ``` This is even better. The address computation is just pointer increments. Let me use this pattern. For the general case, I'll keep the v2 style. Let me write the full v3. Actually, one more idea: we can process multiple rows of H per thread using loop tiling, but H_out is already large enough. Another crucial point: For K=3, the reference is `nn.Conv2d(..., kernel_size=(3,1), ...)`. cudnn might already use a very efficient algorithm for this. The speedup might be modest. However, cudnn has overhead from algorithm selection, workspace allocation, etc. Our kernel avoids all that. For small batch/channel, direct kernel might be faster because cudnn overhead dominates. But with N=64, C=8, H=W=512, cudnn overhead might be amortized. Actually, for these sizes, cudnn should be quite fast. Let me think about what else we can do. Wait, since it's a depthwise conv with kernel width=1, cuDNN might not have a highly optimized kernel for this asymmetric shape and might fall back to a generic implementation or use im2col + GEMM internally, which could be suboptimal. Our direct kernel, being specifically designed for this 1D sliding window, might outperform cuDNN. Let me also note: in PyTorch, nn.Conv2d for depthwise often uses the `cudnn_convolution` path, but depthwise convolutions might also be dispatched to `miopen_depthwise_convolution` on AMD or similar on NVIDIA. On NVIDIA, cuDNN has `CUDNN_CONVOLUTION_FWD_ALGO_IMPLICIT_PRECOMP_GEMM` and others. For small channel counts, cuDNN might use `IMPLICIT_GEMM` or even `WINOGRAD`. But for groups=in_channels=8 with kernel (3,1), Winograd might not apply. I think a custom direct kernel has a good chance of winning. Let me write v3 with the improved fast path and submit. I'll also make sure general case is correct. ```cpp template <typename scalar_t> __launch_bounds__(256) __global__ void depthwise_conv2d_k1_kernel_v3( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation, bool bias_flag) { int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * W_out; if (tid >= total) return; int nc = tid / W_out; int w = tid % W_out; int n = nc / C; int c = nc % C; const scalar_t* input_nc = input + ((n * C + c) * H) * W; scalar_t* output_ncw = output + ((n * C + c) * H_out) * W_out + w; scalar_t wgt[7]; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { wgt[kh] = weight[c * K + kh]; } scalar_t b = bias_flag ? bias[c] : static_cast<scalar_t>(0); int w_in = w * stride - padding; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) { #pragma unroll 4 for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { output_ncw[h * W_out] = b; } return; } if (K == 3 && stride == 1 && padding == 0 && dilation == 1) { const scalar_t* in_ptr = input_nc + w_in; scalar_t* out_ptr = output_ncw; scalar_t w0 = wgt[0]; scalar_t w1 = wgt[1]; scalar_t w2 = wgt[2]; scalar_t v0 = __ldg(in_ptr); in_ptr += W; scalar_t v1 = __ldg(in_ptr); in_ptr += W; scalar_t v2 = __ldg(in_ptr); in_ptr += W; #pragma unroll 8 for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { *out_ptr = b + v0 * w0 + v1 * w1 + v2 * w2; out_ptr += W_out; v0 = v1; v1 = v2; if (h + 3 < H) { v2 = __ldg(in_ptr); in_ptr += W; } } } else { for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { scalar_t acc = b; int h_in_start = h * stride - padding; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_curr = h_in_start + kh * dilation; if (h_curr >= 0 && h_curr < H) { acc += __ldg(input_nc + h_curr * W + w_in) * wgt[kh]; } } output_ncw[h * W_out] = acc; } } } ``` Wait, the general case still has the loop `for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh)` which isn't compile-time unrolled. To reduce latency, we can try to move some computations to be shared across threads or use vector instructions. Actually, a much simpler approach: use warp-level tile where all threads in a warp process the SAME output column together but different h coordinates. Each thread in a warp could handle ~16 output pixels. This reduces the thread's instruction count by a factor of ~32, while still keeping the same total work per output column. And because all threads in a warp share the same input read pattern, they can broadcast values through warp shuffles or they just read their own lines and we get coalescing. Actually, if all threads in a warp handle different h values but the same w and c and n, then when reading from the same input column, they read from different rows. Those might be in the same cache line if adjacent rows are contiguous in memory. For NCHW, rows are contiguous in memory since W comes after H. So adjacent h addresses are separated by W (512 elements * 4 bytes = 2048 bytes). A cache line is typically 128 bytes. So adjacent h addresses are 2048 bytes apart - that's 16 cache lines apart. So adjacent h reads don't share cache lines. But cache lines are loaded into L1 anyway and each warp reads 512 elements * 4 bytes every step. If each thread handles a different h, and they collectively cover a range, say thread t handles h from t*start to (t+1)*start - 1, then within a warp, consecutive threads handle consecutive h values. Their memory accesses are separated by W*stride_H elements in the input. So memory is not coalesced across threads in a warp. Each thread issues a separate cache line request. Actually, within a warp, if thread t is reading input[h_t][w_in], and h_t are different, then the addresses are not consecutive. The address for thread t is input_base + h_t * W + w_in. If h_t differ by values other than small increments that happen to fall within a cache line, each thread generates a separate memory transaction. This is bad for memory throughput. So the best approach is to have each thread operate on consecutive h values so that reads are sequential and reuse the sliding window. But then each thread has too many instructions. Alternative: each thread processes a small tile of output, say 8x1 or 16x1, but we use interleaving so that consecutive threads in a warp process consecutive h values. For example, thread i in a warp handles h = i, i+32, i+64, ... . Then when all threads in a warp read for step j, they read from input[i+j*32][w_in] for i=0..31. The addresses are base + (j*32)*W + i*W. These are spaced by W=512 elements = 2048 bytes. Still not coalesced. Actually, if consecutive threads in a warp read addresses that differ by W elements, this is not contiguous, so it's not coalesced. Each thread generates a 32-byte or 128-byte transaction, but because the addresses are spread far apart, they can't be combined. So the basic per-thread sequential approach actually has good memory behavior but poor instruction-level parallelism. Tensor cores could help but for k=3 that's overkill. Let me now write a refined version with clean code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> at::Tensor depthwise_conv2d_k1_forward( const at::Tensor& input, const at::Tensor& weight, const at::Tensor& bias, int64_t stride, int64_t padding, int64_t dilation, bool bias_flag); """ cuda_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <ATen/cuda/CUDAContext.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <typename scalar_t> __launch_bounds__(256) __global__ void depthwise_conv2d_k1_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation, bool bias_flag) { int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * W_out; if (tid >= total) return; int nc = tid / W_out; int w = tid % W_out; int n = nc / C; int c = nc % C; const scalar_t* input_nc = input + (((n * C) + c) * H) * W; scalar_t* output_ncw = output + (((n * C) + c) * H_out) * W_out + w; scalar_t b = bias_flag ? bias[c] : static_cast<scalar_t>(0.0); int w_in = w * stride - padding; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) { // Entire column is padding for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { output_ncw[h * W_out] = b; } return; } if (K == 3 && stride == 1 && padding == 0 && dilation == 1) { // Optimized sliding window for K=3, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1 scalar_t w0 = weight[c * K + 0]; scalar_t w1 = weight[c * K + 1]; scalar_t w2 = weight[c * K + 2]; const scalar_t* in_ptr = input_nc + w_in; scalar_t* out_ptr = output_ncw; scalar_t v0 = __ldg(in_ptr); in_ptr += W; scalar_t v1 = __ldg(in_ptr); in_ptr += W; scalar_t v2 = __ldg(in_ptr); in_ptr += W; for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { *out_ptr = b + v0 * w0 + v1 * w1 + v2 * w2; out_ptr += W_out; v0 = v1; v1 = v2; if (h + 3 < H) { v2 = __ldg(in_ptr); in_ptr += W; } } } else if (padding == 0) { // No boundary checks needed on H for (int h = 0; h < H_out; ++h) { scalar_t acc = b; int h_in_start = h * stride; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_curr = h_in_start + kh * dilation; acc += __ldg(input_nc + h_curr * W + w_in) * weight[c * K + kh]; } output_ncw[h * W_out] = acc; } } else { // General case with top/bottom boundary checks int h_min = (padding + stride - 1) / stride; if (h_min < 0) h_min = 0; if (h_min > H_out) h_min = H_out; int h_max = (H + padding - (K - 1) * dilation + stride - 1) / stride; if (h_max < 0) h_max = 0; if (h_max > H_out) h_max = H_out; // Top edge: some kernel pixels are above the input for (int h = 0; h < h_min; ++h) { scalar_t acc = b; int h_in_start = h * stride - padding; for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_curr = h_in_start + kh * dilation; if (h_curr >= 0 && h_curr < H) { acc += __ldg(input_nc + h_curr * W + w_in) * weight[c * K + kh]; } } output_ncw[h * W_out] = acc; } // Interior: all kernel pixels are inside the input for (int h = h_min; h < h_max; ++h) { scalar_t acc = b; int h_in_start = h * stride - padding; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_curr = h_in_start + kh * dilation; acc += __ldg(input_nc + h_curr * W + w_in) * weight[c * K + kh]; } output_ncw[h * W_out] = acc; } // Bottom edge: some kernel pixels are below the input for (int h = h_max; h < H_out; ++h) { scalar_t acc = b; int h_in_start = h * stride - padding; for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_curr = h_in_start + kh * dilation; if (h_curr >= 0 && h_curr < H) { acc += __ldg(input_nc + h_curr * W + w_in) * weight[c * K + kh]; } } output_ncw[h * W_out] = acc; } } } at::Tensor depthwise_conv2d_k1_forward( const at::Tensor& input, const at::Tensor& weight, const at::Tensor& bias, int64_t stride, int64_t padding, int64_t dilation, bool bias_flag) { int N = input.size(0); int C = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int K = weight.size(2); int H_out = (H + 2 * static_cast<int>(padding) - static_cast<int>(dilation) * (K - 1) - 1) / static_cast<int>(stride) + 1; int W_out = (W + 2 * static_cast<int>(padding) - 1) / static_cast<int>(stride) + 1; auto output = at::empty({N, C, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); int total = N * C * W_out; const int block_size = 256; int grid_size = (total + block_size - 1) / block_size; AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(input.scalar_type(), "depthwise_conv2d_k1", [&] { const scalar_t* input_ptr = input.data_ptr<scalar_t>(); const scalar_t* weight_ptr = weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(); const scalar_t* bias_ptr = bias_flag ? bias.data_ptr<scalar_t>() : nullptr; scalar_t* output_ptr = output.data_ptr<scalar_t>(); depthwise_conv2d_k1_kernel<scalar_t><<<grid_size, block_size, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( input_ptr, weight_ptr, bias_ptr, output_ptr, N, C, H, W, H_out, W_out, K, static_cast<int>(stride), static_cast<int>(padding), static_cast<int>(dilation), bias_flag ); }); return output; } """ ``` This looks cleaner. My kernel uses a flat grid over (N*C*W_out) threads, where W_out = (512 - 1)/1 + 1 = 512. So total threads = 64 * 8 * 512 = 262144. With block size 256, grid size = 1024 blocks. For the specific test case, K=3, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1, so the fast path is taken. Key ideas for improvement: 1. Coalesce memory accesses by storing output in column major per thread. Currently output_ncw[h * W_out] = ... has stride W_out=512 between writes. If consecutive threads process consecutive w, then consecutive threads write to positions differing by W_out, not consecutive addresses. This is actually bad for coalescing! - Wait - thread tid writes to output[(((n*C)+c)*H_out)*W_out + w], where w = tid % W_out. - For consecutive threads, w increments by 1. So threads 0,1,2,... write to addresses that are consecutive! - But within each thread, it writes output_ncw[0], output_ncw[W_out], output_ncw[2*W_out], etc. These are separated by 512 elements = 2KB. This is sequential writes with a large stride. Not coalesced at all. Actually, that's a really important issue. Each thread writes H_out=512 scalar_t values, each separated by 512*4=2048 bytes. These are strided writes that go to different memory pages and are not coalesced. Better: have each thread compute a small tile of several output pixels that are spatially close. But since each thread currently computes a full column (all H for one W), the writes are strided. Alternatively, we could have threads within a block cooperate to compute a tile. But a simpler approach: each thread computes a few adjacent w values, interleaving the reads and writes to maintain better coalescing. Wait, actually let's think about this more carefully. A warp of 32 threads processes w values 0..31 (for the same n,c). Each thread writes: - thread 0: addr + 0, addr + W_out, addr + 2*W_out, ... - thread 1: addr + 1, addr + 1 + W_out, addr + 1 + 2*W_out, ... When all 32 threads write simultaneously at h=0, they write to addr+0, addr+1, ..., addr+31 — fully coalesced! Then at h=1, they write to addr+W_out, addr+W_out+1, ..., addr+W_out+31 — also fully coalesced! So actually the writes ARE coalesced because consecutive threads write to consecutive memory addresses at each h step. Wait, that's only if they execute in lockstep. But within each thread, the loop over h has a dependency (v0=v1, v1=v2, read new v2), so all threads should be roughly in sync. The writes are coalesced. So memory coalescing is actually quite good already. 2. Instruction-level parallelism: The kernel has good ILP because each iteration does several FMAs and the sliding window unrolls nicely. 3.
